\u003E","post_canonical":"[quote=Functional]Let $\\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$ denote the set of all positive rational numbers. Determine all functions $f:\\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}\\to \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$ satisfying $$f(x^2f(y)^2)=f(x)^2f(y)$$ for all $x,y\\in\\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$[\/quote]\n\n[b]Solution[\/b] Plugging $x = \\frac{1}{f(1)}, y = 1$ gives us $f\\left(\\frac{1}{f(1)}\\right) = 1$. Plugging $x, \\frac{1}{f(1)}$ gives us $f(x^2) = f(x)^2$. This gives us $f(xf(y))^2 = f(x)^2f(y)$ or $f(y)$ is a square of a rational for all positive rationals $q$. Now plug $f_1(x) = \\sqrt{f(x)}$ to get $f_1(xf_1(y)^2)^4 = f_1(x)^4f_1(y)^2$ which again gives $f_1(y)$ is a square of a rational for all positive rationals $q$. This eventually gives us that $f(x)^{\\frac{1}{2^n}}$ is a rational which is false for large $n$, therefore $f(x) = 1$ is the only solution which works. $y$. We now claim:\n$$\\underbrace{f \\circ \\cdots \\circ f}_{\\text{n times}}(x)=f^n(x)=c \\cdot \\left(\\frac{f(x)}{c} \\right)^{1\/2^{n-1}}$$\nfor all $n \\geq 1$. This clearly holds for $n=1$. We go by induction. For $n \\geq 2$:\n$$f^{n}(x)=f(f(f^{n-2}(x)))=\\left(c f(f^{n-2}(x)) \\right)^{1\/2}=\\left(c f^{n-1}(x) \\right)^{1\/2}=\\left(c^2 \\left(\\frac{f(x)}{c} \\right)^{1\/2^{n-2}} \\right)^{1\/2}$$\nand expanding out shows the result is true by induction.\n\nBut then we have:\n$$\\left(\\frac{f(x)}{c} \\right)^{1\/2^{n-1}}=\\frac{f^{n}(x)}{c} \\in \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$$\nfor all $n$. is a rational number when taken $\\frac{1}{2^k}$-th power is $1$. \nLet $x=f(z)$ at the given assertion, then $f(f(z)^2f(y)^2)=f(f(z))^2f(y)=f(f(y)^2)f(z)$. Hence there exists some $c \\in \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$ such that $\\frac{f(f(x))^2}{f(x)}=c$ for any $x$. Now consider $g(x)=\\frac{f(x)}{c}$, then $g(f(x))^2=g(x)$ for any $x$. This means that $g(x), \\sqrt{g(x)}, \\cdots, \\sqrt[2^k]{g(x)}, \\cdots$ are all rationals, giving that $g$ is constantly $1$. From $P\\left(1, 1\\right)$, we have\n\t\\begin{align*}\n\t\tf\\left(c^2\\right) \u0026= c^3.\n\t\\end{align*}\nNow, from $P\\left(c^2, 1\\right)$, we have\n\t\\begin{align*}\n\t\tf\\left(c^6\\right) \u0026= c^7,\n\t\\end{align*}\nwhile from $P\\left(1, c^2\\right)$, we have\n\t\\begin{align*}\n\t\tf\\left(c^6\\right) \u0026= c^5.\n\t\\end{align*}\nHence, $c^5 = c^7$, and since $c \\in \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E 0}$, $c = 1$. Therefore, $f\\left(1\\right) = 1$. Now, $P\\left(x, 1\\right)$ implies\n\t\\begin{align*}\n\t\tf\\left(x^2\\right) \u0026= f\\left(x\\right)^2.\n\t\\end{align*}\nFrom $P\\left(1, x\\right)$, we have\n\t\\begin{align*}\n\t\tf\\left(f\\left(x\\right)^2\\right) \u0026= f\\left(x\\right) \\\\\n\t\tf\\left(f\\left(x\\right)\\right)^2 \u0026= f\\left(x\\right).\n\t\\end{align*}\nTherefore, if $k$ is in the image of $f$, so is $k^{\\frac{1}{2}}$. Iterating, we find that in fact $k^{\\frac{1}{2^\\ell}}$ is in the image of $f$ for all positive integers $\\ell$. Now, suppose $k \\neq 1$. Then, for $\\ell$ large enough, $k^{\\frac{1}{2^\\ell}}$ will not be rational, contradiction. Hence, we must have $k = 1$. This immediately implies that $f \\equiv 1$ identically, concluding the proof. I claim that $f(x)=1$ is the only solution, which is easily verifiable, and I will do this by showing that $f(x)$ is a perfect $2^n$th power of a rational for all $x\\in \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$. We proceed with induction on $n$. The base case is easy: taking $P(1,x)$ gives $$f(f(x))^2=f(1)^2f(x)\\implies f(x)=\\left(\\frac{f(f(x))}{f(1)}\\right)^2$$Since $f$ maps $\\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$ to $\\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$, we must have $f(x)$ is a perfect square of a rational number for all $x\\in \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$. Now assume the claim is true for some $n=k\\geq 1$. Because of the assumption, we can set a function $g(x)$ such that $g(x)^{2^k}=f(x)$ over the same domain and range. We have $$g^{2^k}(x)=\\left(\\frac{g^{2^k}(f(x))}{g^{2^k}(1)}\\right)^2=\\left(\\frac{g(f(x))}{g(1)}\\right)^{2^{k+1}}$$ Taking the $2^k$th root gives $$g(x)=\\left(\\frac{g(f(x))}{g(1)}\\right)^2$$ Since $g(x)$ is over $\\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$, the LHS must be a perfect square of a rational for all $x$. Due to the definition of $g$ this means that $f(x)$ is a perfect $2^{k+1}$th power of some function over the positive rationals as well, completing the induction. Since $f(x)$ is a perfect $2^n$th power for any positive integer $n$, we must have that Substituting $(x,a)$ yields $f(x^2)=f(x)^2$. This allows us to rewrite the given FE as $f(xf(y))^2=f(x)^2f(y)$. This implies that $f(y)$ is always a square. This means that we can write $f(x)=f_1(x)^2$ for some $f_1$. substituting this in we get $f_1(xf_1(y)^2)^4=f_1(x)^4f_1(y)^2$. Now we can take the square root of this equation, and again find that $f_1(y)$ must be a square for all $y$. Repeating this argument, $f(y)$ has to be a perfect $2$nd, $4$th, $\\ldots$ power. The only number satisfying this is $1$, so $f(x)=1$ is the only \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$.\n\nLet $c = f(1)$, and denote by $P(x, y)$ the assertion $f(x^2f(y)^2) = f(x)^2f(y)$. Then consider the following:\n\\begin{align*}\nP(1\/c, 1): \u0026\\implies f \\left(\\frac{1}{c} \\right) = 1 \\\\\nP(x, 1\/c): \u0026\\implies f(x^2) = f(x)^2 \\implies f(1) = 1.\\\\\n\u0026\\implies f(y) = \\left[\\frac{f(xf(y))}{f(x)} \\right]^2. \\text{ Let } Q(x, y) \\text{ denote this assertion.} \\\\\nQ(1, y): \u0026\\implies f(y) = f(f(y))^2; \\text{ and } f(x) = f(f(x))^2 \\\\\n\u0026\\implies \\frac{f(y)}{f(x)} = \\left[\\frac{f^{2}(y)}{f^{(2)}(x)} \\right]^2 = \\left[\\frac{f^{(3)}(y)}{f^{(3)}(x)} \\right]^4 = \\cdots = \\left[\\frac{f^{(k)}(y)}{f^{(k)}(x)} \\right]^{2^{k - 1}} = c_0. \\\\\n\\end{align*}\nNow $c_0 = b^{2^{k - 1}}$, for some $b \\in \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$, and in particular, if $f$ is to be well defined we require that $b \\in [0, 1]$. Consider taking $x = \\frac{1}{f(y)}$ to fnd,\n\\begin{align*}\nf(1) \u0026= f(y) \\cdot f\\left(\\frac{1}{f(y)} \\right)^2\n\\end{align*}\nPlugging in $y = 1$ this means,\n\\begin{align*}\nf\\left( \\frac{1}{f(1)} \\right) = 1\n\\end{align*}\nHowever we may also note if we take $y = \\frac{1}{f(1)}$,\n\\begin{align*}\nf(x^2) = f(x)^2\n\\end{align*}\nFrom here take this identity and plug it back into the original equation to find,\n\\begin{align*}\nf(xf(y))^2 \u0026= f(x)^2f(y) \\iff \\frac{f(xf(y))^2}{f(x)^2} = f(y)\n\\end{align*}\nThen $f(x)$ is always the square of a rational number. However then as,\n\\begin{align*}\nf(xf(y)) = f(x)\\sqrt{f(y)}\n\\end{align*}\nwe require $f(y)$ to always be the fourth power of a rational number. However then this implies,\n\\begin{align*}\n\\frac{f(xf(y))}{f(x)} = \\sqrt{f(y)}\n\\end{align*}\nwhich in turn gives $\\sqrt{f(y)}$ is the perfect fourth power of a rational, which forces $f(y)$ to be a perfect eighth power of a rational. Continuing this infinite descent $f(y)$ is a perfect power of a rational for each of the powers of $2$. This forces $f(y) = 1$ as [\/b][\/color]The only solution is $f(x)\\equiv 1$.\nLet $P(x,y)$ denote the assertion: $$f(x^2f(y)^2)=f(x)^2f(y)$$\n$P\\left( \\frac{1}{f(1)},1\\right) \\implies f\\left( \\frac{1}{f(1)} \\right)=1 \\implies \\exists \\alpha$ such that $f(\\alpha)=1$.\n$$P(x,\\alpha) \\implies \\boxed{f(x^2)=f(x)^2} \\cdots(i)$$\n\n$P(1,x^2) \\implies f(1f(x)^2)^2=f(1)^2f(x)^2\\implies f(f(x)^2)=f(1)f(x)$\n$P(1,x) \\implies f(f(x)^2)=f(1)^2.f(x)$\nFrom these two equations, we get that $f(1)=1$.\n$P(1,x) \\implies f(x)=f(f(x))^2$\n\nLet $F_i=f(f(f(..._{\\text{i times}}))$\nTherefore, $\\forall x$, we have $f(x)=F_2(x)^2$.\nSubstituting this value of $f(x)$ in the original FE, we get:\n$$F_2(x^2F_2(y)^2)=F_2(x)^2F_2(y)$$\nDoing same thing recursively, we get that $F_2(x)=F_4(x)^2 \\implies f(x)=F_4(x)^{2^2}$\nContinuing by infinite descent, we gradually get that $f(x)=F_{2^i}(x)^{2^i}$ for all $i \\in \\mathbb{N}$\nThus, we can have no other solution, other than $\\boxed{f(x)\\equiv 1}$, and we\u0027re done! :maybe: We claim the answer is $f(x)=1$, which clearly works. We now show it is the only possible solution.\n\n[b][color=#00f]Claim:[\/color][\/b] $f\\left(\\frac{1}{f(x)}\\right)=\\sqrt{\\frac{f(1)}{f(x)}}$\n[i]Proof:[\/i] \nUsing $P\\left(\\frac{1}{f(y)},y\\right)$, we get:\n\\[f(1)=f\\left(\\frac{1}{f(y)}\\right)^2\\cdot f(y),\\]\nas desired $\\square$\n\n[b][color=#00f]Claim:[\/color][\/b] $f(x^2)=f(x)^2$\n[i]Proof:[\/i]\nWe now use $P(1,y)$, which can be written as:\n\\[f(f(y)^2)=f(1)^2\\cdot f(y).\\]\nBy the previous claim, this is also:\n\\[f(f(y)^2)=\\sqrt{f(1)}\\cdot f(y).\\]\nTherefore:\n\\[f(1)^{3\/2}=1,\\]\n\\[f(1)=1.\\]\nThen, if we use $P(x,1)$, we get the desired result $\\square$\n\nWhen we substitute this back, we get:\n\\[f(xf(y))^2=f(x)^2\\cdot f(y).\\]\nThis means that $f(y)=g(y)^2$, such that $g:\\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}\\to \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$. Applying this, we get:\n\\[g(xg(y)^2)^4=g(x)^4g(y)^2,\\]\n\\[g(xg(y)^2)^2=g(x)^2g(y).\\]\nAs a result, $g$ must also be a square of a function $q: \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}\\to \\mathbb{Q}_{\u003E0}$, leading to an infinite descent. Therefore, $f$ must be a perfect $2^i$ power as $i$ approaches infinity, implying that $f$ must be $1$ 1.\\]\n\nThen, $P(x,\\tfrac{1}{f(1)^2})$ gives\n\n\\[f(x^2) = f(x)^2.\\]\n\nPlug this into the original equation to see that\n\n\\[f(xf(y))^2 = f(x)^2f(y)\\]\n\nand then let $x = f(y)$, which simplifies to\n\n\\[f(f(y))^2 = f(y).\\]\n\nHence, if $b$ is in the range of $f$ such that $f(a)=b$, we see that $f(f(a))^2 = f(b)^2 = f(a) = b$, so $\\sqrt{b}$ is also in the range of $f$; infinite A new academic year is a new opportunity to train for the upcoming competition season.  Our [url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/woot]Worldwide Online Olympiad Training (WOOT)[\/url] classes begin the first week of September, so be sure to take advantage of the only program where you learn the skills and strategies necessary to succeed at the Olympiad Level.  All of this year\u2019s International Mathematical Olympiad team members are AoPS alums and earned 5 gold medals and 1 silver medal, bringing home a first place finish!  Did you know that John Berman, this year\u2019s IMO Team leader for the US, is also an AoPS alum!?\n\nBelow is a list of the different WOOT programs and the competitions they cover.\n\n[list][*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-math#level-1]MathWOOT Level 1[\/url]:  Designed for AIME qualifiers ready to make the jump to Olympiads. \n[*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-math#level-2]MathWOOT Level 2[\/url]:  Designed for Olympiad qualifiers ready to increase their scores in national and international Olympiads.\n[*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-code]CodeWOOT[\/url]: USACO, IOI\n[*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-physics]PhysicsWOOT[\/url]: USAPhO, SIN, IPhO, F=ma exam\n[*][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/woot-chem]ChemWOOT[\/url]:  USNCO, IChO[\/list]\nFor those not quite ready for CodeWOOT and are interested in coding competitions, check out our [b][url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/cs-bronze]brand new USACO Bronze course[\/b][\/url]!  To be eligible, students should already be comfortable enough with C++, Java, or Python to write simple programs using basic concepts like arrays, maps\/sets, if statements, and for loops. Note that either Java or Python is sufficient for the USACO Bronze and Silver levels (AoPS offers Python courses), but USACO Gold and above (and most programming contests) essentially require C++, and the IOI only supports C++.  \n\nKeep in mind some other important dates coming up fast!\n[list][*][b]AMC 10\/12 competitions are right around the corner![\/b] Take advantage of our accelerated [url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/maa-amc10]AMC 10[\/url] and [url=https:\/\/artofproblemsolving.com\/school\/course\/maa-amc12]AMC 12[\/url] Problem Series courses which run from October until right before the competitions begin! This contest is another method for qualifying for AIME. As an extra bonus, students can use the comments they receive on their USAMTS solutions to hone their skills for Olympiad level contests! The Round 1 problems will be available at [url=http:\/\/www.usamts.org\/contest\/problems]www.usamts.org\/contest\/problems[\/url]. Students should submit solutions to the Round 1 problems by mid-October.\n[*][url=https:\/\/math.mit.edu\/research\/highschool\/primes\/program\/apply.html]Admissions for MIT Primes[\/url] opens on October 1st!  MIT PRIMES is a free, year-long program, in which high school students work on individual and group research projects and participate in reading groups under the guidance of academic mentors. <script id="footer-tpl-email-dialog" type="text/x-handlebars-template">
	<div id="mail-dialog">

<script id="footer-tpl-email-confirm" type="text/x-handlebars-template">
	<div id="mail-dialog-confirm">
		<h2>Email Sent</h2>
		<p>Thank you for your email. We will respond to it soon.</p>

<script>
	// Try to prevent sending email multiple times
	var last_email = '';

	$( function() {
		$( ".aops-contact" ).on( "click", displayFooterEmailDialog );

		$( "body" ).on( "click", "#footer-submit-btn", submitFooterEmail );
		$( "body" ).on( "click", "#footer-cancel-btn",  cancelFooterEmail );
	} );

	function displayFooterEmailDialog() {
		AoPS.Ui.Modal.show( {
			body : document.getElementById( "footer-tpl-email-dialog" ).innerHTML,
			max_width : '600px',
			max_height : '100%',
			scrollable : true
		} );
	}


	/**
	 * Submit email
	 *
	 * Note: Copy does not work in non-production mode because Mailer::quick() is
	 * used which does not do force send.
	 */
	function submitFooterEmail() {
		var obj = {
			action : "email",
			email : $( "#email" ).val(),
			subject : $( "#subject" ).val(),
			message : $( "#message" ).val(),
			copy : $( "#email-copy" ).is( ":checked" )
		};

		if ( obj.email === last_email ) {
			return;
		}
		last_email = obj.email;

		// If this still happens multiple times, we have
		// something wrong with the internal ajax call.
		AoPS.Ui.Modal.closeAllModals();
		$.post( "/m/contact/ajax.php", obj, function( data ) {
			if ( data.error_code ) {
				$( "#email-error" ).html( data.error_msg ? data.error_msg : data.error_code ).show();
			} else {
				AoPS.Ui.Modal.show( {
					body : document.getElementById( "footer-tpl-email-confirm" ).innerHTML,
					max_width : '600px',
					max_height : '100%',
					scrollable : true
				} );
			}
		}, "json" );
	}
	function cancelFooterEmail() {
		AoPS.Ui.Modal.closeAllModals();
	}
</script>
